   <div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body"> 
    <div id="siteSub" class="noprint">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>

      <div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">
       Country comprising the mainland of the Australian continent
      </div>

      </div>

      <p><b>Australia</b>, officially the <b>Commonwealth of Australia</b>, is a country comprising the mainland of the <a href="/wiki/Australia_(continent)" title="Australia (continent)">Australian continent</a>, the island of <a href="/wiki/Tasmania" title="Tasmania">Tasmania</a>, and numerous <a href="/wiki/List_of_islands_of_Australia" title="List of islands of Australia">smaller islands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[13]</a></sup> It is the largest country in <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a> and the world's <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_dependencies_by_area" title="List of countries and dependencies by area">sixth-largest country</a>. Australia's population of nearly 26 million,<sup id="cite_ref-popclock_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-popclock-10">[7]</a></sup> in an area of 7,617,930 square kilometres (2,941,300&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi),<sup id="cite_ref-Size_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Size-18">[14]</a></sup> is highly <a href="/wiki/Urbanization" title="Urbanization">urbanised</a> and heavily concentrated on the eastern seaboard.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[15]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Canberra" title="Canberra">Canberra</a> is the nation's capital, while the <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_Australia_by_population" title="List of cities in Australia by population">largest city</a> is <a href="/wiki/Sydney" title="Sydney">Sydney</a>, and other major <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_Australia" title="List of cities in Australia">metropolitan areas</a> are <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brisbane" title="Brisbane">Brisbane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perth" title="Perth">Perth</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Adelaide" title="Adelaide">Adelaide</a>. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Indigenous_Australians" title="Indigenous Australians">Indigenous Australians</a> inhabited the continent for about <a href="/wiki/Early_human_migrations#Near_Oceania" title="Early human migrations">65,000 years</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ClarksonJacobs2017_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ClarksonJacobs2017-20">[16]</a></sup> prior to the <a href="/wiki/European_maritime_exploration_of_Australia" title="European maritime exploration of Australia">first arrival</a> of <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">Dutch</a> explorers in the early 17th century, who named it <a href="/wiki/New_Holland_(Australia)" title="New Holland (Australia)">New Holland</a>. In 1770, Australia's eastern half was claimed by <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Great Britain</a> and initially <a href="/wiki/History_of_Australia_(1788%E2%80%931850)" title="History of Australia (1788–1850)">settled</a> through <a href="/wiki/Penal_transportation" title="Penal transportation">penal transportation</a> to the colony of <a href="/wiki/New_South_Wales" title="New South Wales">New South Wales</a> from 26 January 1788, a date which became Australia's <a href="/wiki/Australia_Day" title="Australia Day">national day</a>. The population grew steadily in subsequent decades, and by the time of an 1850s <a href="/wiki/Australian_gold_rushes" title="Australian gold rushes">gold rush</a>, most of the continent had been explored by European settlers and an additional five self-governing <a href="/wiki/Crown_colony" title="Crown colony">crown colonies</a> established. On 1 January 1901, the six colonies <a href="/wiki/Federation_of_Australia" title="Federation of Australia">federated</a>, forming the Commonwealth of Australia. Australia has since maintained a stable <a href="/wiki/Liberal_democracy" title="Liberal democracy">liberal democratic</a> political system that functions as a <a href="/wiki/Federalism" title="Federalism">federal</a> <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_system" title="Parliamentary system">parliamentary</a> <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a>, comprising <a href="/wiki/States_and_territories_of_Australia" title="States and territories of Australia">six states and ten territories</a>. </p>
      <p>Australia is the oldest,<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[17]</a></sup> flattest,<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[18]</a></sup> and driest inhabited continent,<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[20]</a></sup> with the least fertile <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soils</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[22]</a></sup> It is a <a href="/wiki/Megadiverse_countries" title="Megadiverse countries">megadiverse country</a>, and its size gives it a wide variety of landscapes and climates, with <a href="/wiki/Deserts_of_Australia" title="Deserts of Australia">deserts</a> in the centre, tropical <a href="/wiki/Forests_of_Australia" title="Forests of Australia">rainforests</a> in the north-east, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_mountains_in_Australia" title="List of mountains in Australia">mountain ranges</a> in the south-east. Australia generates its income from various sources, including <a href="/wiki/Mining_in_Australia" title="Mining in Australia">mining-related exports</a>, <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications_in_Australia" title="Telecommunications in Australia">telecommunications</a>, <a href="/wiki/Banking_in_Australia" title="Banking in Australia">banking</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing_in_Australia" title="Manufacturing in Australia">manufacturing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/International_students_in_Australia" title="International students in Australia">international education</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[25]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Australia is a highly <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">developed country</a>, with the world's <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(nominal)" title="List of countries by GDP (nominal)">twelfth-largest economy</a>. It has a <a href="/wiki/World_Bank_high-income_economy" title="World Bank high-income economy">high-income economy</a>, with the world's <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(nominal)_per_capita" title="List of countries by GDP (nominal) per capita">tenth-highest per capita income</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[26]</a></sup> Australia is a <a href="/wiki/Regional_power" title="Regional power">regional power</a>, and has the world's <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_military_expenditures" title="List of countries by military expenditures">thirteenth-highest military expenditure</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[27]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Immigration_to_Australia" title="Immigration to Australia">Immigrants</a> account for 30% of the country's population,<sup id="cite_ref-auto1_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-32">[28]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/List_of_sovereign_states_and_dependent_territories_by_immigrant_population" title="List of sovereign states and dependent territories by immigrant population">highest proportion</a> among major <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western</a> nations.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[29]</a></sup> Having the sixth-highest <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_Human_Development_Index" title="List of countries by Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a>, and the eighth-highest ranked <a href="/wiki/Democracy_Index" title="Democracy Index">democracy</a> globally, Australia <a href="/wiki/International_rankings_of_Australia" title="International rankings of Australia">ranks highly</a> in quality of life, health, education, <a href="/wiki/Economic_freedom" title="Economic freedom">economic freedom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Civil_liberties" title="Civil liberties">civil liberties</a>, and political rights,<sup id="cite_ref-World_Audit_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-World_Audit-34">[30]</a></sup> with all its major cities faring exceptionally in global comparative <a href="/wiki/Most_livable_cities" title="Most livable cities">livability</a> surveys.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[31]</a></sup> It is a member of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, the <a href="/wiki/G20" title="G20">G20</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a>, the <a href="/wiki/ANZUS" title="ANZUS">ANZUS</a>, the <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a>, the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organization" title="World Trade Organization">WTO</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Asia-Pacific_Economic_Cooperation" title="Asia-Pacific Economic Cooperation">APEC</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Islands_Forum" title="Pacific Islands Forum">Pacific Islands Forum</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/ASEAN" title="ASEAN">ASEAN</a> + 6 mechanism. </p>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Name_of_Australia" title="Name of Australia">Name of Australia</a>
      </div> 
      <p><span class="anchor" id="Etymology"></span> </p>
      <p>The name <i>Australia</i> (pronounced <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span> in <a href="/wiki/Australian_English" title="Australian English">Australian English</a><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[32]</a></sup>) is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Terra_Australis" title="Terra Australis">Terra Australis</a></i> ("southern land"), a name used for a hypothetical continent in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a> since ancient times.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[33]</a></sup> When Europeans first began visiting and mapping Australia in the 17th century, the name <i>Terra Australis</i> was naturally applied to the new territories.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[N 5]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Until the early 19th century, Australia was best known as "<a href="/wiki/New_Holland_(Australia)" title="New Holland (Australia)">New Holland</a>", a name first applied by the Dutch explorer <a href="/wiki/Abel_Tasman" title="Abel Tasman">Abel Tasman</a> in 1644 (as <i>Nieuw-Holland</i>) and subsequently anglicised. <i>Terra Australis</i> still saw occasional usage, such as in scientific texts.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[N 6]</a></sup> The name <i>Australia</i> was popularised by the explorer <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Flinders" title="Matthew Flinders">Matthew Flinders</a>, who said it was "more agreeable to the ear, and an assimilation to the names of the other great portions of the Earth".<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[39]</a></sup> Several famous early cartographers also made use of the word Australia on maps. <a href="/wiki/Gerardus_Mercator" title="Gerardus Mercator">Gerardus Mercator</a> used the phrase <i>climata australia</i> on his double cordiform map of the world of 1538, as did <a href="/wiki/Gemma_Frisius" title="Gemma Frisius">Gemma Frisius</a>, who was Mercator's teacher and collaborator, on his own cordiform wall map in 1540. Australia appears in a book on astronomy by Cyriaco Jacob zum Barth published in Frankfurt am Main in 1545.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[40]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The first time that <i>Australia</i> appears to have been officially used was in April 1817, when Governor <a href="/wiki/Lachlan_Macquarie" title="Lachlan Macquarie">Lachlan Macquarie</a> acknowledged the receipt of Flinders' charts of Australia from <a href="/wiki/Henry_Bathurst,_3rd_Earl_Bathurst" title="Henry Bathurst, 3rd Earl Bathurst">Lord Bathurst</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[41]</a></sup> In December 1817, Macquarie recommended to the <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Office" title="Colonial Office">Colonial Office</a> that it be formally adopted.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[42]</a></sup> In 1824, the <a href="/wiki/British_Admiralty" class="mw-redirect" title="British Admiralty">Admiralty</a> agreed that the continent should be known officially by that name.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[43]</a></sup> The first official published use of the new name came with the publication in 1830 of <i>The Australia Directory</i> by the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Hydrographic_Office" title="United Kingdom Hydrographic Office">Hydrographic Office</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[44]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Colloquial names for Australia include "<a href="/wiki/Name_of_Australia#Oz" title="Name of Australia">Oz</a>" and "the Land Down Under" (usually shortened to just "<a href="/wiki/Down_Under" title="Down Under">Down Under</a>"). Other epithets include "the Great Southern Land", "<a href="/wiki/The_Lucky_Country" title="The Lucky Country">the Lucky Country</a>", "the Sunburnt Country", and "the Wide Brown Land". The latter two both derive from <a href="/wiki/Dorothea_Mackellar" title="Dorothea Mackellar">Dorothea Mackellar</a>'s 1908 poem "<a href="/wiki/My_Country" title="My Country">My Country</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[45]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Australia" title="History of Australia">History of Australia</a>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_Australians" title="Indigenous Australians">Indigenous Australians</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Bradshaw_rock_paintings.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Bradshaw_rock_paintings.jpg/220px-Bradshaw_rock_paintings.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Bradshaw_rock_paintings.jpg/330px-Bradshaw_rock_paintings.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Bradshaw_rock_paintings.jpg/440px-Bradshaw_rock_paintings.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="683"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Bradshaw_rock_paintings.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Aboriginal_rock_art" class="mw-redirect" title="Aboriginal rock art">Aboriginal rock art</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Kimberley_(Western_Australia)" title="Kimberley (Western Australia)">Kimberley</a> region of Western Australia
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Human habitation of the Australian continent is known to have begun at least 65,000 years ago,<sup id="cite_ref-Nunn2018_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nunn2018-52">[46]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FaganDurrani2018_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FaganDurrani2018-53">[47]</a></sup> with the migration of people by <a href="/wiki/Land_bridge" title="Land bridge">land bridges</a> and short sea-crossings from what is now <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Oppenheimer2013_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oppenheimer2013-54">[48]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Madjedbebe" title="Madjedbebe">Madjedbebe</a> rock shelter in <a href="/wiki/Arnhem_Land" title="Arnhem Land">Arnhem Land</a> is recognised as the oldest site showing the presence of humans in Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-Gilligan2018_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gilligan2018-55">[49]</a></sup> The oldest human remains found are the <a href="/wiki/Lake_Mungo_remains" title="Lake Mungo remains">Lake Mungo remains</a>, which have been dated to around 41,000 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-TunizGillespie2016_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TunizGillespie2016-56">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Castillo2015_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Castillo2015-57">[51]</a></sup> These people were the ancestors of modern Indigenous Australians.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[52]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aboriginal_Australian" class="mw-redirect" title="Aboriginal Australian">Aboriginal Australian</a> culture is one of the oldest continual cultures on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[53]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>At the time of first European contact, most Indigenous Australians were <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherers</a> with complex economies and societies.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SáenzEmbrick2015_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SáenzEmbrick2015-61">[55]</a></sup> Recent archaeological finds suggest that a population of 750,000 could have been sustained.<sup id="cite_ref-pop_abs_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pop_abs-62">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[57]</a></sup> Indigenous Australians have an <a href="/wiki/Oral_tradition" title="Oral tradition">oral culture</a> with spiritual values based on reverence for the land and a belief in the <a href="/wiki/Dreamtime" class="mw-redirect" title="Dreamtime">Dreamtime</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Galván2014_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Galván2014-64">[58]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Torres_Strait_Islanders" title="Torres Strait Islanders">Torres Strait Islanders</a>, ethnically <a href="/wiki/Melanesia" title="Melanesia">Melanesian</a>, obtained their livelihood from seasonal horticulture and the resources of their reefs and seas.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[59]</a></sup> The northern coasts and waters of Australia were <a href="/wiki/Makassan_contact_with_Australia" title="Makassan contact with Australia">visited sporadically</a> by <a href="/wiki/Makassar_people" title="Makassar people">Makassan</a> fishermen from what is now <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[60]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_arrival">European arrival</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/European_exploration_of_Australia" title="European exploration of Australia">European exploration of Australia</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Emanuel_Phillips_Fox_Captain_Cook_Botany_Bay.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A painting of Captain James Cook landing at Botany Bay, New South Wales" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Emanuel_Phillips_Fox_Captain_Cook_Botany_Bay.jpg/220px-Emanuel_Phillips_Fox_Captain_Cook_Botany_Bay.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Emanuel_Phillips_Fox_Captain_Cook_Botany_Bay.jpg/330px-Emanuel_Phillips_Fox_Captain_Cook_Botany_Bay.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Emanuel_Phillips_Fox_Captain_Cook_Botany_Bay.jpg/440px-Emanuel_Phillips_Fox_Captain_Cook_Botany_Bay.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="936"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Emanuel_Phillips_Fox_Captain_Cook_Botany_Bay.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/James_Cook" title="James Cook">Captain Cook</a> landing at Botany Bay, 1770
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The first recorded European sighting of the Australian mainland, and the first recorded European landfall on the Australian continent, are attributed to the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_people" title="Dutch people">Dutch</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BarberBarnes2013_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BarberBarnes2013-67">[61]</a></sup> The first ship and crew to chart the Australian coast and meet with Aboriginal people was the <i><a href="/wiki/Duyfken" title="Duyfken">Duyfken</a></i> captained by Dutch navigator, <a href="/wiki/Willem_Janszoon" title="Willem Janszoon">Willem Janszoon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SmithBurke2007_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SmithBurke2007-68">[62]</a></sup> He sighted the coast of <a href="/wiki/Cape_York_Peninsula" title="Cape York Peninsula">Cape York Peninsula</a> in early 1606, and made landfall on 26 February 1606 at the <a href="/wiki/Pennefather_River" title="Pennefather River">Pennefather River</a> near the modern town of <a href="/wiki/Weipa,_Queensland" class="mw-redirect" title="Weipa, Queensland">Weipa</a> on Cape York.<sup id="cite_ref-dhm233_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dhm233-69">[63]</a></sup> Later that year, Spanish explorer <a href="/wiki/Lu%C3%ADs_Vaz_de_Torres" title="Luís Vaz de Torres">Luís Vaz de Torres</a> sailed through, and navigated, <a href="/wiki/Torres_Strait" title="Torres Strait">Torres Strait</a> islands.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[64]</a></sup> The Dutch charted the whole of the western and northern coastlines and named the island continent "New Holland" during the 17th century, and although no attempt at settlement was made,<sup id="cite_ref-dhm233_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dhm233-69">[63]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shipwrecks_of_Western_Australia#Notable_wrecks" title="Shipwrecks of Western Australia">a number of shipwrecks</a> left men either stranded or, as in the case of the <i><a href="/wiki/Batavia_(1628_ship)" title="Batavia (1628 ship)">Batavia</a></i> in 1629, marooned for mutiny and murder, thus becoming the first Europeans to permanently inhabit the continent.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[65]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_Dampier" title="William Dampier">William Dampier</a>, an English explorer and privateer, landed on the north-west coast of New Holland in 1688 (while serving as a crewman under pirate Captain <a href="/wiki/John_Read_(pirate)" title="John Read (pirate)">John Read</a><sup id="cite_ref-Baer_–_Pirates_of_the_British_Isles_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baer_–_Pirates_of_the_British_Isles-72">[66]</a></sup>) and again in 1699 on a return trip.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[67]</a></sup> In 1770, James Cook sailed along and mapped the east coast, which he named New South Wales and claimed for Great Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-GoucherWalton2013_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GoucherWalton2013-74">[68]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>With the loss of its American colonies in 1783, the British Government sent a fleet of ships, the "<a href="/wiki/First_Fleet" title="First Fleet">First Fleet</a>", under the command of Captain <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Phillip" title="Arthur Phillip">Arthur Phillip</a>, to establish a new <a href="/wiki/Penal_colony" title="Penal colony">penal colony</a> in New South Wales. A camp was set up and the <a href="/wiki/Union_flag" class="mw-redirect" title="Union flag">Union flag</a> raised at <a href="/wiki/Sydney_Cove" title="Sydney Cove">Sydney Cove</a>, <a href="/wiki/Port_Jackson" title="Port Jackson">Port Jackson</a>, on 26 January 1788,<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Egan2003_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Egan2003-76">[70]</a></sup> a date which later became Australia's national day, <a href="/wiki/Australia_Day" title="Australia Day">Australia Day</a>. Most early <a href="/wiki/Convicts_in_Australia" title="Convicts in Australia">convicts</a> were <a href="/wiki/Penal_transportation" title="Penal transportation">transported</a> for petty crimes and assigned as labourers or servants upon arrival. While the majority settled into colonial society once <a href="/wiki/Emancipist" title="Emancipist">emancipated</a>, convict rebellions and uprisings were also staged, but invariably suppressed under martial law. The 1808 <a href="/wiki/Rum_Rebellion" title="Rum Rebellion">Rum Rebellion</a>, the only successful armed takeover of government in Australia, instigated a two-year period of military rule.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[71]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The indigenous population declined for 150 years following settlement, mainly due to infectious disease.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[72]</a></sup> Thousands more died as a result of <a href="/wiki/Australian_frontier_wars" title="Australian frontier wars">frontier conflict</a> with settlers.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[73]</a></sup> A government policy of "assimilation" beginning with the <i><a href="/wiki/Aboriginal_Protection_Act_1869" title="Aboriginal Protection Act 1869">Aboriginal Protection Act 1869</a></i> resulted in the removal of many Aboriginal children from their families and communities—referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Stolen_Generations" title="Stolen Generations">Stolen Generations</a> — a practice which also contributed to the decline in the indigenous population.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[74]</a></sup> As a result of the <a href="/wiki/Australian_referendum,_1967_(Aboriginals)" class="mw-redirect" title="Australian referendum, 1967 (Aboriginals)">1967 referendum</a>, the Federal government's power to enact special laws with respect to a particular race was extended to enable the making of laws with respect to Aboriginals.<sup id="cite_ref-Edwards2004_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards2004-81">[75]</a></sup> Traditional ownership of land ("<a href="/wiki/Native_title_in_Australia" title="Native title in Australia">native title</a>") was not recognised in law until 1992, when the <a href="/wiki/High_Court_of_Australia" title="High Court of Australia">High Court of Australia</a> held in <i><a href="/wiki/Mabo_v_Queensland_(No_2)" title="Mabo v Queensland (No 2)">Mabo v Queensland (No 2)</a></i> that the legal doctrine that Australia had been <i><a href="/wiki/Terra_nullius" title="Terra nullius">terra nullius</a></i> ("land belonging to no one") did not apply to Australia at the time of British settlement.<sup id="cite_ref-Davison_pp._5-7,_402_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davison_pp._5-7,_402-82">[76]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial_expansion">Colonial expansion</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:PortArthurPenitentiary.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A calm body of water is in the foreground. The shoreline is about 200 metres away. To the left, close to the shore, are three tall gum trees; behind them on an incline are ruins, including walls and watchtowers of light-coloured stone and brick, what appear to be the foundations of walls, and grassed areas. To the right lie the outer walls of a large rectangular four-storey building dotted with regularly spaced windows. Forested land rises gently to a peak several kilometres back from the shore." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/PortArthurPenitentiary.jpg/220px-PortArthurPenitentiary.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/PortArthurPenitentiary.jpg/330px-PortArthurPenitentiary.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/PortArthurPenitentiary.jpg/440px-PortArthurPenitentiary.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="667"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:PortArthurPenitentiary.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Tasmania's <a href="/wiki/Port_Arthur,_Tasmania" title="Port Arthur, Tasmania">Port Arthur</a> penal settlement is one of eleven UNESCO World Heritage-listed <a href="/wiki/Australian_Convict_Sites" title="Australian Convict Sites">Australian Convict Sites</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The expansion of British control over other areas of the continent began in the early 19th century, initially confined to coastal regions. A settlement was established in <a href="/wiki/Van_Diemen%27s_Land" title="Van Diemen's Land">Van Diemen's Land</a> (present-day <a href="/wiki/Tasmania" title="Tasmania">Tasmania</a>) in 1803, and it became a separate colony in 1825.<sup id="cite_ref-Davison_pp464-5_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davison_pp464-5-83">[77]</a></sup> In 1813, <a href="/wiki/Gregory_Blaxland" title="Gregory Blaxland">Gregory Blaxland</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Lawson_(explorer)" title="William Lawson (explorer)">William Lawson</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Wentworth" title="William Wentworth">William Wentworth</a> crossed the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Mountains_(New_South_Wales)" title="Blue Mountains (New South Wales)">Blue Mountains</a> west of <a href="/wiki/Sydney" title="Sydney">Sydney</a>, opening the interior to European settlement.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[78]</a></sup> The British claim was extended to the whole Australian continent in 1827 when Major <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Lockyer" title="Edmund Lockyer">Edmund Lockyer</a> established a settlement on <a href="/wiki/King_George_Sound" class="mw-redirect" title="King George Sound">King George Sound</a> (modern-day <a href="/wiki/Albany,_Western_Australia" title="Albany, Western Australia">Albany</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[79]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Swan_River_Colony" title="Swan River Colony">Swan River Colony</a> (present-day <a href="/wiki/Perth" title="Perth">Perth</a>) was established in 1829, evolving into the largest Australian colony by area, <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Davison_p678_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davison_p678-86">[80]</a></sup> In accordance with population growth, separate colonies were carved from parts of New South Wales: <a href="/wiki/South_Australia" title="South Australia">South Australia</a> in 1836, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> in 1841, <a href="/wiki/Victoria_(Australia)" title="Victoria (Australia)">Victoria</a> in 1851, and <a href="/wiki/Queensland" title="Queensland">Queensland</a> in 1859.<sup id="cite_ref-Davison_p464_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davison_p464-87">[81]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Northern_Territory" title="Northern Territory">Northern Territory</a> was excised from South Australia in 1911.<sup id="cite_ref-Davison_p470_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davison_p470-88">[82]</a></sup> South Australia was founded as a "free province" — it was never a penal colony.<sup id="cite_ref-Davison_p598_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davison_p598-89">[83]</a></sup> Western Australia was also founded "free" but later accepted <a href="/wiki/Convict_era_of_Western_Australia" title="Convict era of Western Australia">transported convicts</a>, the last of which arrived in 1868, decades after transportation had ceased to the other colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[84]</a></sup> In the mid-19th century, explorers such as <a href="/wiki/Burke_and_Wills_expedition" title="Burke and Wills expedition">Burke and Wills</a> went further inland to determine its agricultural potential and answer scientific questions.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[85]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>A <a href="/wiki/Australian_gold_rushes" title="Australian gold rushes">series of gold rushes</a> beginning in the early 1850s led to an influx of new migrants from <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Australians" title="Chinese Australians">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> and <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">continental Europe</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-JuppJupp2001_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JuppJupp2001-92">[86]</a></sup> and also spurred outbreaks of <a href="/wiki/Bushranger" title="Bushranger">bushranging</a> and civil unrest; the latter peaked in 1854 when <a href="/wiki/Ballarat" title="Ballarat">Ballarat</a> miners launched the <a href="/wiki/Eureka_Rebellion" title="Eureka Rebellion">Eureka Rebellion</a> against gold license fees.<sup id="cite_ref-Davison_pp227-9_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davison_pp227-9-93">[87]</a></sup> Between 1855 and 1890, the six colonies individually gained <a href="/wiki/Responsible_government" title="Responsible government">responsible government</a>, managing most of their own affairs while remaining part of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Davison_p556_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davison_p556-94">[88]</a></sup> The Colonial Office in London retained control of some matters, notably foreign affairs<sup id="cite_ref-Davison_p138-9679_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davison_p138-9679-95">[89]</a></sup> and defence.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[90]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nationhood">Nationhood</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Anzac2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photo of an ANZAC memorial with an elderly man playing the bugle. Rows of people are seated behind the memorial. Many small white crosses with red poppies have been stuck into the lawn in rows on either side of the memorial." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Anzac2.jpg/220px-Anzac2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Anzac2.jpg/330px-Anzac2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Anzac2.jpg/440px-Anzac2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="935" data-file-height="802"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Anzac2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Last_Post" title="Last Post">Last Post</a> is played at an <a href="/wiki/Anzac_Day" title="Anzac Day">Anzac Day</a> ceremony in <a href="/wiki/Port_Melbourne,_Victoria" title="Port Melbourne, Victoria">Port Melbourne</a>, Victoria. Similar ceremonies are held in many suburbs and towns.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>On 1 January 1901, <a href="/wiki/Federation_of_Australia" title="Federation of Australia">federation of the colonies</a> was achieved after a decade of planning, consultation and voting.<sup id="cite_ref-Davison_pp243-4_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davison_pp243-4-97">[91]</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/1907_Imperial_Conference" title="1907 Imperial Conference">1907 Imperial Conference</a>, Australia and the other self-governing British colonies were given the status of "<a href="/wiki/Dominion" title="Dominion">dominion</a>" within the British Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-dominionstatus_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dominionstatus-98">[92]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[93]</a></sup> The Federal Capital Territory (later renamed the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Capital_Territory" title="Australian Capital Territory">Australian Capital Territory</a>) was formed in 1911 as the location for the future federal capital of Canberra. <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a> was the temporary seat of government from 1901 to 1927 while Canberra was being constructed.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[94]</a></sup> The Northern Territory was transferred from the control of the South Australian government to the federal parliament in 1911.<sup id="cite_ref-Souter2012_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Souter2012-101">[95]</a></sup> Australia became the colonial ruler of the <a href="/wiki/Territory_of_Papua" title="Territory of Papua">Territory of Papua</a> (which had initially been annexed by Queensland in 1883<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[96]</a></sup>) in 1902 and of the <a href="/wiki/Territory_of_New_Guinea" title="Territory of New Guinea">Territory of New Guinea</a> (formerly <a href="/wiki/German_New_Guinea" title="German New Guinea">German New Guinea</a>) in 1920. The two were unified as the <a href="/wiki/Territory_of_Papua_and_New_Guinea" title="Territory of Papua and New Guinea">Territory of Papua and New Guinea</a> in 1949 and gained independence from Australia in 1975.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[99]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1914, Australia joined Britain in fighting World War I, with support from both the outgoing <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Liberal_Party" title="Commonwealth Liberal Party">Commonwealth Liberal Party</a> and the incoming <a href="/wiki/Australian_Labor_Party" title="Australian Labor Party">Australian Labor Party</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[100]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[101]</a></sup> Australians took part in many of the major battles fought on the <a href="/wiki/Western_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Western Front (World War I)">Western Front</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[102]</a></sup> Of about 416,000 who served, about 60,000 were killed and another 152,000 were wounded.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[103]</a></sup> Many Australians regard the defeat of the <a href="/wiki/Australian_and_New_Zealand_Army_Corps" title="Australian and New Zealand Army Corps">Australian and New Zealand Army Corps</a> (ANZACs) at <a href="/wiki/Gallipoli_Campaign" class="mw-redirect" title="Gallipoli Campaign">Gallipoli</a> as the birth of the nation — its first major military action.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[105]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Kokoda_Track_campaign" title="Kokoda Track campaign">Kokoda Track campaign</a> is regarded by many as an analogous nation-defining event during World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[106]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Britain's <a href="/wiki/Statute_of_Westminster_1931" title="Statute of Westminster 1931">Statute of Westminster 1931</a> formally ended most of the constitutional links between Australia and the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>. Australia <a href="/wiki/Statute_of_Westminster_Adoption_Act_1942" title="Statute of Westminster Adoption Act 1942">adopted it</a> in 1942,<sup id="cite_ref-Davison_p609_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davison_p609-113">[107]</a></sup> but it was backdated to 1939 to confirm the validity of legislation passed by the Australian Parliament during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[108]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[109]</a></sup> The shock of Britain's <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Singapore" title="Battle of Singapore">defeat in Asia</a> in 1942, followed soon after by the <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Darwin" title="Bombing of Darwin">bombing of Darwin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Air_raids_on_Australia,_1942%E2%80%9343" title="Air raids on Australia, 1942–43">other Japanese attacks</a>, led to a widespread belief in Australia that <a href="/wiki/Proposed_Japanese_invasion_of_Australia_during_World_War_II" title="Proposed Japanese invasion of Australia during World War II">an invasion was imminent</a>, and a shift towards the <a href="/wiki/Australia%E2%80%93United_States_relations" title="Australia–United States relations">United States as a new ally</a> and protector.<sup id="cite_ref-Davison_pp22-3_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davison_pp22-3-116">[110]</a></sup> Since 1951, Australia has been a formal military ally of the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, under the <a href="/wiki/ANZUS" title="ANZUS">ANZUS</a> treaty.<sup id="cite_ref-Davison_p30_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davison_p30-117">[111]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>After World War II, Australia encouraged immigration from mainland <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>. Since the 1970s and following the abolition of the <a href="/wiki/White_Australia_policy" title="White Australia policy">White Australia policy</a>, immigration from <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> and elsewhere was also promoted.<sup id="cite_ref-Davison_pp338-6,_681-2_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davison_pp338-6,_681-2-118">[112]</a></sup> As a result, Australia's demography, culture, and self-image were transformed.<sup id="cite_ref-Davison_p442–379_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davison_p442–379-119">[113]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Australia_Act_1986" title="Australia Act 1986">Australia Act 1986</a></i> severed the remaining constitutional ties between Australia and the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[114]</a></sup> In a <a href="/wiki/1999_Australian_republic_referendum" title="1999 Australian republic referendum">1999 referendum</a>, 55% of voters and a majority in every state rejected a proposal to become a <a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">republic</a> with a president appointed by a two-thirds vote in both Houses of the Australian Parliament. There has been an increasing focus in foreign policy on ties with other <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Rim" title="Pacific Rim">Pacific Rim</a> nations while maintaining close ties with Australia's traditional allies and trading partners.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[115]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography_and_environment">Geography and environment</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Australia" title="Geography of Australia">Geography of Australia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Environment_of_Australia" title="Environment of Australia">Environment of Australia</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_issues_in_Australia" title="Environmental issues in Australia">Environmental issues in Australia</a>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_characteristics">General characteristics</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Reliefmap_of_Australia.png" class="image"><img alt="Map showing the topography of Australia, showing some elevation in the west and very high elevation in mountains in the southeast" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Reliefmap_of_Australia.png/290px-Reliefmap_of_Australia.png" decoding="async" width="290" height="266" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Reliefmap_of_Australia.png/435px-Reliefmap_of_Australia.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Reliefmap_of_Australia.png/580px-Reliefmap_of_Australia.png 2x" data-file-width="996" data-file-height="914"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Reliefmap_of_Australia.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Topographic map of Australia. Dark green represents the lowest elevation and dark brown the highest
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Surrounded by the Indian and Pacific oceans,<sup id="cite_ref-Southern_Ocean_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Southern_Ocean-123">[N 7]</a></sup> Australia is separated from Asia by the <a href="/wiki/Arafura_Sea" title="Arafura Sea">Arafura</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timor_Sea" title="Timor Sea">Timor</a> seas, with the <a href="/wiki/Coral_Sea" title="Coral Sea">Coral Sea</a> lying off the Queensland coast, and the <a href="/wiki/Tasman_Sea" title="Tasman Sea">Tasman Sea</a> lying between Australia and <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>. The world's smallest continent<sup id="cite_ref-NatlGeo_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NatlGeo-124">[117]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_outlying_territories_by_total_area" class="mw-redirect" title="List of countries and outlying territories by total area">sixth largest country by total area</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-125">[118]</a></sup> Australia—owing to its size and isolation—is often dubbed the "island continent"<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[119]</a></sup> and is sometimes considered the <a href="/wiki/List_of_islands_by_area" title="List of islands by area">world's largest island</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[120]</a></sup> Australia has 34,218&nbsp;km (21,262&nbsp;mi) of coastline (excluding all offshore islands),<sup id="cite_ref-Coast_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coast-128">[121]</a></sup> and claims an extensive <a href="/wiki/Exclusive_economic_zone" title="Exclusive economic zone">Exclusive Economic Zone</a> of 8,148,250 square kilometres (3,146,060&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi). This exclusive economic zone does not include the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Antarctic_Territory" title="Australian Antarctic Territory">Australian Antarctic Territory</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[122]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Mainland Australia lies between latitudes <a href="/wiki/9th_parallel_south" title="9th parallel south">9°</a> and <a href="/wiki/44th_parallel_south" title="44th parallel south">44° South</a>, and longitudes <a href="/wiki/112th_meridian_east" title="112th meridian east">112°</a> and <a href="/wiki/154th_meridian_east" title="154th meridian east">154° East</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-lat_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lat-130">[123]</a></sup> Australia's size gives it a wide variety of landscapes, with tropical <a href="/wiki/Rainforest" title="Rainforest">rainforests</a> in the north-east, mountain ranges in the south-east, south-west and east, and desert in the centre.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-131">[124]</a></sup> The desert or semi-arid land commonly known as the <a href="/wiki/Outback" title="Outback">outback</a> makes up by far the largest portion of land.<sup id="cite_ref-portrait_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-portrait-132">[125]</a></sup> Australia is the driest inhabited continent; its annual rainfall averaged over continental area is less than 500 mm.<sup id="cite_ref-bomclim_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bomclim-133">[126]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_dependencies_by_population_density" title="List of countries and dependencies by population density">population density</a> is 3.2 inhabitants per square kilometre, although a large proportion of the population lives along the temperate south-eastern coastline.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[127]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Heron_Island.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Heron_Island.jpg/220px-Heron_Island.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Heron_Island.jpg/330px-Heron_Island.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Heron_Island.jpg/440px-Heron_Island.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="681"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Heron_Island.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Heron_Island_(Queensland)" title="Heron Island (Queensland)">Heron Island</a>, a coral cay in the southern Great Barrier Reef
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Barrier_Reef" title="Great Barrier Reef">Great Barrier Reef</a>, the world's largest coral reef,<sup id="cite_ref-UNEP_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNEP-135">[128]</a></sup> lies a short distance off the north-east coast and extends for over 2,000&nbsp;km (1,200&nbsp;mi). <a href="/wiki/Mount_Augustus_National_Park" title="Mount Augustus National Park">Mount Augustus</a>, claimed to be the world's largest monolith,<sup id="cite_ref-Monolith_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Monolith-136">[129]</a></sup> is located in Western Australia. At 2,228&nbsp;m (7,310&nbsp;ft), <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kosciuszko" title="Mount Kosciuszko">Mount Kosciuszko</a> is the highest mountain on the Australian mainland. Even taller are <a href="/wiki/Mawson_Peak" title="Mawson Peak">Mawson Peak</a> (at 2,745&nbsp;m (9,006&nbsp;ft)), on the remote Australian <a href="/wiki/States_and_territories_of_Australia" title="States and territories of Australia">external territory</a> of <a href="/wiki/Heard_Island_and_McDonald_Islands" title="Heard Island and McDonald Islands">Heard Island</a>, and, in the Australian Antarctic Territory, <a href="/wiki/Mount_McClintock" title="Mount McClintock">Mount McClintock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mount_Menzies" title="Mount Menzies">Mount Menzies</a>, at 3,492&nbsp;m (11,457&nbsp;ft) and 3,355&nbsp;m (11,007&nbsp;ft) respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[130]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Eastern Australia is marked by the <a href="/wiki/Great_Dividing_Range" title="Great Dividing Range">Great Dividing Range</a>, which runs parallel to the coast of <a href="/wiki/Queensland" title="Queensland">Queensland</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_South_Wales" title="New South Wales">New South Wales</a> and much of <a href="/wiki/Victoria_(Australia)" title="Victoria (Australia)">Victoria</a>. The name is not strictly accurate, because parts of the range consist of low hills, and the highlands are typically no more than 1,600&nbsp;m (5,200&nbsp;ft) in height.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnson2009p202_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson2009p202-138">[131]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Australian_temperate_forests" title="Eastern Australian temperate forests">coastal uplands</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Brigalow_Belt" title="Brigalow Belt">belt of Brigalow grasslands</a> lie between the coast and the mountains, while inland of the dividing range are large areas of grassland and shrubland.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnson2009p202_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson2009p202-138">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[132]</a></sup> These include the <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Australia_temperate_savanna" title="Southeast Australia temperate savanna">western plains</a> of New South Wales, and the <a href="/wiki/Mitchell_Grass_Downs" title="Mitchell Grass Downs">Mitchell Grass Downs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mulga_Lands" title="Mulga Lands">Mulga Lands</a> of inland Queensland.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[133]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[134]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[135]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[136]</a></sup> The northernmost point of the mainland is the tropical <a href="/wiki/Cape_York_Peninsula" title="Cape York Peninsula">Cape York Peninsula</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-lat_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lat-130">[123]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Uluru,_helicopter_view,_cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Uluru%2C_helicopter_view%2C_cropped.jpg/220px-Uluru%2C_helicopter_view%2C_cropped.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Uluru%2C_helicopter_view%2C_cropped.jpg/330px-Uluru%2C_helicopter_view%2C_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Uluru%2C_helicopter_view%2C_cropped.jpg/440px-Uluru%2C_helicopter_view%2C_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2876" data-file-height="1884"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Uluru,_helicopter_view,_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Uluru" title="Uluru">Uluru</a> in the semi-arid region of Central Australia
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The landscapes of the <a href="/wiki/Top_End" title="Top End">Top End</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Country" title="Gulf Country">Gulf Country</a>—with their tropical climate—include forest, <a href="/wiki/Woodland" title="Woodland">woodland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grassland" title="Grassland">grassland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rainforest" title="Rainforest">rainforest</a> and <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">desert</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[137]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[138]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[139]</a></sup> At the north-west corner of the continent are the sandstone cliffs and gorges of <a href="/wiki/Kimberley_(Western_Australia)" title="Kimberley (Western Australia)">The Kimberley</a>, and below that the <a href="/wiki/Pilbara" title="Pilbara">Pilbara</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Plains_tropical_savanna" title="Victoria Plains tropical savanna">Victoria Plains tropical savanna</a> lies south of the <a href="/wiki/Kimberley_tropical_savanna" title="Kimberley tropical savanna">Kimberly</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arnhem_Land_tropical_savanna" title="Arnhem Land tropical savanna">Arnhem Land</a> savannas, forming a transition between the coastal savannas and the interior deserts.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[140]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[141]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[142]</a></sup> At the heart of the country are the <a href="/wiki/Central_Ranges_xeric_scrub" title="Central Ranges xeric scrub">uplands of central Australia</a>. Prominent features of the centre and south include <a href="/wiki/Uluru" title="Uluru">Uluru</a> (also known as Ayers Rock), the famous sandstone monolith, and the inland <a href="/wiki/Simpson_Desert" title="Simpson Desert">Simpson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tirari-Sturt_stony_desert" class="mw-redirect" title="Tirari-Sturt stony desert">Tirari and Sturt Stony</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gibson_Desert" title="Gibson Desert">Gibson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Great_Sandy-Tanami_desert" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Sandy-Tanami desert">Great Sandy, Tanami</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Great_Victoria_Desert" title="Great Victoria Desert">Great Victoria</a> deserts, with the famous <a href="/wiki/Nullarbor_Plain" title="Nullarbor Plain">Nullarbor Plain</a> on the southern coast.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[143]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[144]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-152">[145]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[146]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Western_Australian_mulga_shrublands" title="Western Australian mulga shrublands">Western Australian mulga shrublands</a> lie between the interior deserts and Mediterranean-climate <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Australia_(ecoregion)" class="mw-redirect" title="Southwest Australia (ecoregion)">Southwest Australia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_152-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-152">[145]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[147]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Australia" title="Geology of Australia">Geology of Australia</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Ausgeolbasic.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Ausgeolbasic.jpg/260px-Ausgeolbasic.jpg" decoding="async" width="260" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Ausgeolbasic.jpg/390px-Ausgeolbasic.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Ausgeolbasic.jpg/520px-Ausgeolbasic.jpg 2x" data-file-width="918" data-file-height="845"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Ausgeolbasic.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Basic geological regions of Australia, by age.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Lying on the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Australian_Plate" title="Indo-Australian Plate">Indo-Australian Plate</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Australian_mainland" class="mw-redirect" title="Australian mainland">mainland of Australia</a> is the lowest and most primordial landmass on Earth with a relatively stable geological history.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[148]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[149]</a></sup> The landmass includes virtually all known <a href="/wiki/List_of_rock_types" title="List of rock types">rock types</a> and from all geological time periods spanning over 3.8 billion years of the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Earth" title="History of Earth">Earth's history</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Pilbara_Craton" title="Pilbara Craton">Pilbara Craton</a> is one of only two pristine <a href="/wiki/Archean" title="Archean">Archaean</a> 3.6–2.7 Ga (billion years ago) crusts identified on the Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[150]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Having been part of all major <a href="/wiki/Supercontinent" title="Supercontinent">supercontinents</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Australia_(continent)" title="Australia (continent)">Australian continent</a> began to form after the breakup of <a href="/wiki/Gondwana" title="Gondwana">Gondwana</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Permian" title="Permian">Permian</a>, with the separation of the continental landmass from the <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">African</a> continent and <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>. It separated from <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> over a prolonged period beginning in the <a href="/wiki/Permian" title="Permian">Permian</a> and continuing through to the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[151]</a></sup> When the <a href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Last glacial period">last glacial period</a> ended in about 10,000 BC, rising sea levels formed <a href="/wiki/Bass_Strait" title="Bass Strait">Bass Strait</a>, separating <a href="/wiki/Tasmania" title="Tasmania">Tasmania</a> from the mainland. Then between about 8,000 and 6,500 BC, the lowlands in the north were flooded by the sea, separating <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Aru_Islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Aru Islands">Aru Islands</a>, and the mainland of Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[152]</a></sup> The Australian continent is moving toward <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> at the rate of 6 to 7 centimetres a year.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[153]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Australian mainland's <a href="/wiki/Continental_crust" title="Continental crust">continental crust</a>, excluding the thinned margins, has an average thickness of 38<span class="nowrap">&nbsp;</span>km, with a range in thickness from 24 km to 59 km.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[154]</a></sup> Australia's geology can be divided into several main sections, showcasing that the continent grew from west to east: the Archaean <a href="/wiki/Craton" title="Craton">cratonic</a> shields found mostly in the west, <a href="/wiki/Proterozoic" title="Proterozoic">Proterozoic</a> <a href="/wiki/Orogeny" title="Orogeny">fold belts</a> in the centre and <a href="/wiki/Phanerozoic" title="Phanerozoic">Phanerozoic</a> <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_basins" class="mw-redirect" title="Sedimentary basins">sedimentary basins</a>, metamorphic and <a href="/wiki/Igneous_rocks" class="mw-redirect" title="Igneous rocks">igneous rocks</a> in the east.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[155]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Australian mainland and Tasmania are situated in the middle of the <a href="/wiki/Tectonic_plate" class="mw-redirect" title="Tectonic plate">tectonic plate</a> and have no active volcanoes,<sup id="cite_ref-ag_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ag-163">[156]</a></sup> but due to passing over the <a href="/wiki/East_Australia_hotspot" title="East Australia hotspot">East Australia hotspot</a>, recent volcanism has occurred during the <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Newer_Volcanics_Province" title="Newer Volcanics Province">Newer Volcanics Province</a> of western Victoria and southeastern South Australia. Volcanism also occurs in the island of New Guinea (considered geologically as part of the Australian continent), and in the Australian external territory of <a href="/wiki/Heard_Island_and_McDonald_Islands" title="Heard Island and McDonald Islands">Heard Island and McDonald Islands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[157]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/List_of_earthquakes_in_Australia" title="List of earthquakes in Australia">Seismic activity</a> in the Australian mainland and Tasmania is also low, with the greatest number of fatalities having occurred in the <a href="/wiki/1989_Newcastle_earthquake" title="1989 Newcastle earthquake">1989 Newcastle earthquake</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[158]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Australia" title="Climate of Australia">Climate of Australia</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Australia_K%C3%B6ppen.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Australia_K%C3%B6ppen.svg/260px-Australia_K%C3%B6ppen.svg.png" decoding="async" width="260" height="290" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Australia_K%C3%B6ppen.svg/390px-Australia_K%C3%B6ppen.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Australia_K%C3%B6ppen.svg/520px-Australia_K%C3%B6ppen.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1260" data-file-height="1404"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Australia_K%C3%B6ppen.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen climate types</a> of Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[159]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The climate of Australia is significantly influenced by ocean currents, including the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean_Dipole" title="Indian Ocean Dipole">Indian Ocean Dipole</a> and the <a href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o%E2%80%93Southern_Oscillation" title="El Niño–Southern Oscillation">El Niño–Southern Oscillation</a>, which is correlated with periodic <a href="/wiki/Drought_in_Australia" title="Drought in Australia">drought</a>, and the seasonal tropical low-pressure system that produces <a href="/wiki/Cyclone" title="Cyclone">cyclones</a> in northern Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[160]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[161]</a></sup> These factors cause rainfall to vary markedly from year to year. Much of the northern part of the country has a tropical, predominantly summer-rainfall (<a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-bomclim_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bomclim-133">[126]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Southwest_corner_of_Western_Australia" class="mw-redirect" title="Southwest corner of Western Australia">south-west corner of the country</a> has a <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_climate" title="Mediterranean climate">Mediterranean climate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[162]</a></sup> The south-east ranges from <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_climate" title="Oceanic climate">oceanic</a> (Tasmania and coastal Victoria) to <a href="/wiki/Humid_subtropical" class="mw-redirect" title="Humid subtropical">humid subtropical</a> (upper half of New South Wales), with the highlands featuring <a href="/wiki/Alpine_climate" title="Alpine climate">alpine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Subpolar_oceanic_climate" class="mw-redirect" title="Subpolar oceanic climate">subpolar oceanic climates</a>. The interior is <a href="/wiki/Arid" title="Arid">arid</a> to <a href="/wiki/Semi-arid" class="mw-redirect" title="Semi-arid">semi-arid</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bomclim_133-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bomclim-133">[126]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Driven by <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, average temperatures have risen <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_in_Australia" title="Climate change in Australia">more than 1°C since 1960</a>. Associated changes in rainfall patterns and climate extremes exacerbate existing issues such as drought and <a href="/wiki/Bushfires_in_Australia" title="Bushfires in Australia">bushfires</a>. 2019 was Australia's warmest recorded year,<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[163]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/2019%E2%80%9320_Australian_bushfire_season" title="2019–20 Australian bushfire season">2019–2020 bushfire season</a> was the country's worst <a href="/wiki/List_of_Australian_bushfire_seasons" title="List of Australian bushfire seasons">on record</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[164]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_Australia" title="Greenhouse gas emissions by Australia">Australia's greenhouse gas emissions</a> per capita are among the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_greenhouse_gas_emissions_per_capita" class="mw-redirect" title="List of countries by greenhouse gas emissions per capita">highest in the world</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[165]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Water_restrictions_in_Australia" title="Water restrictions in Australia">Water restrictions</a> are frequently in place in many regions and cities of Australia in response to chronic shortages due to urban population increases and localised drought.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[166]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[167]</a></sup> Throughout much of the continent, <a href="/wiki/Floods_in_Australia" title="Floods in Australia">major flooding</a> regularly follows extended periods of drought, flushing out inland river systems, overflowing dams and inundating large inland flood plains, as occurred throughout Eastern Australia in the early 2010s after the <a href="/wiki/2000s_Australian_drought" title="2000s Australian drought">2000s Australian drought</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[168]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiversity">Biodiversity</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Fauna_of_Australia" title="Fauna of Australia">Fauna of Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flora_of_Australia" title="Flora of Australia">Flora of Australia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fungi_of_Australia" title="Fungi of Australia">Fungi of Australia</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Koala_climbing_tree.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A koala holding onto a eucalyptus tree with its head turned so both eyes are visible" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Koala_climbing_tree.jpg/220px-Koala_climbing_tree.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="216" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Koala_climbing_tree.jpg/330px-Koala_climbing_tree.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Koala_climbing_tree.jpg/440px-Koala_climbing_tree.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1132" data-file-height="1113"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Koala_climbing_tree.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Koala" title="Koala">koala</a> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Eucalyptus" title="Eucalyptus">eucalyptus</a></i> form an iconic Australian pair.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Although most of Australia is semi-arid or desert, the continent includes a diverse range of habitats from <a href="/wiki/Alpine_climate" title="Alpine climate">alpine</a> heaths to <a href="/wiki/Tropical_rainforest" title="Tropical rainforest">tropical rainforests</a>. Fungi typify that diversity—an estimated 250,000 species—of which only 5% have been described—occur in Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[169]</a></sup> Because of the continent's great age, extremely variable weather patterns, and long-term geographic isolation, much of Australia's <a href="/wiki/Biota_(ecology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Biota (ecology)">biota</a> is unique. About 85% of flowering plants, 84% of mammals, more than 45% of <a href="/wiki/List_of_birds_of_Australia" title="List of birds of Australia">birds</a>, and 89% of in-shore, temperate-zone fish are <a href="/wiki/Endemism" title="Endemism">endemic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[170]</a></sup> Australia has at least 755 species of reptile, more than any other country in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[171]</a></sup> Besides Antarctica, Australia is the only continent that developed without <a href="/wiki/Felidae" title="Felidae">feline</a> species. <a href="/wiki/Feral_cats" class="mw-redirect" title="Feral cats">Feral cats</a> may have been introduced in the 17th century by Dutch shipwrecks, and later in the 18th century by European settlers. They are now considered a major factor in the decline and extinction of many vulnerable and endangered native species.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[172]</a></sup> Australia is also one of 17 <a href="/wiki/Megadiverse_countries" title="Megadiverse countries">megadiverse countries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[173]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Forests_of_Australia" title="Forests of Australia">Australian forests</a> are mostly made up of evergreen species, particularly <a href="/wiki/Eucalyptus" title="Eucalyptus">eucalyptus</a> trees in the less arid regions; <a href="/wiki/Acacia" title="Acacia">wattles</a> replace them as the dominant species in drier regions and deserts.<sup id="cite_ref-dfat_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dfat-181">[174]</a></sup> Among well-known <a href="/wiki/Fauna_of_Australia" title="Fauna of Australia">Australian animals</a> are the <a href="/wiki/Monotreme" title="Monotreme">monotremes</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Platypus" title="Platypus">platypus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Echidna" title="Echidna">echidna</a>); a host of <a href="/wiki/Marsupial" title="Marsupial">marsupials</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Kangaroo" title="Kangaroo">kangaroo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Koala" title="Koala">koala</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wombat" title="Wombat">wombat</a>, and birds such as the <a href="/wiki/Emu" title="Emu">emu</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kookaburra" title="Kookaburra">kookaburra</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-dfat_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dfat-181">[174]</a></sup> Australia is home to <a href="/wiki/Animal_attacks_in_Australia" title="Animal attacks in Australia">many dangerous animals</a> including some of the most venomous snakes in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[175]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Dingo" title="Dingo">dingo</a> was introduced by Austronesian people who traded with Indigenous Australians around 3000 <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">BCE</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-savolainen2004_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-savolainen2004-183">[176]</a></sup> Many animal and plant species became extinct soon after first human settlement,<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[177]</a></sup> including the <a href="/wiki/Australian_megafauna" title="Australian megafauna">Australian megafauna</a>; others have disappeared since European settlement, among them the <a href="/wiki/Thylacine" title="Thylacine">thylacine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NW_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NW-185">[178]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[179]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Many of Australia's ecoregions, and the species within those regions, are threatened by human activities and <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species_in_Australia" title="Invasive species in Australia">introduced</a> animal, <a href="/wiki/Chromista" title="Chromista">chromistan</a>, fungal and plant species.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[180]</a></sup> All these factors have led to Australia's having the highest mammal extinction rate of any country in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[181]</a></sup> The federal <i>Environment Protection and Biodiversity Conservation Act 1999</i> is the legal framework for the protection of threatened species.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[182]</a></sup> Numerous <a href="/wiki/Protected_areas_of_Australia" title="Protected areas of Australia">protected areas</a> have been created under the <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_action_plan" title="Biodiversity action plan">National Strategy for the Conservation of Australia's Biological Diversity</a> to protect and preserve unique ecosystems;<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[183]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[184]</a></sup> 65 <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetlands</a> are <a href="/wiki/List_of_Ramsar_sites_in_Australia" title="List of Ramsar sites in Australia">listed</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Ramsar_Convention" title="Ramsar Convention">Ramsar Convention</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[185]</a></sup> and 16 natural <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Sites</a> have been established.<sup id="cite_ref-WHC_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHC-193">[186]</a></sup> Australia was ranked 21st out of 178 countries in the world on the 2018 <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Performance_Index" title="Environmental Performance Index">Environmental Performance Index</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[187]</a></sup> There are more than 1,800 animals and plants on Australia's threatened species list, including more than 500 animals.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[188]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_and_politics">Government and politics</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Australia" class="mw-redirect" title="Government of Australia">Government of Australia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Australia" title="Politics of Australia">Politics of Australia</a>
      </div>
